Mott Center, Detroit, MI 48201, USA. e-mail: ZG91Z2xhc3JAd2F5bmUuZWR1\n        ",[],[804,805,809],{"#text":782},{"p":806},[807],{"#text":808},"This article was submitted to Frontiers in Toxicogenomics, a specialty of Frontiers in Genetics.",{"#text":788},"2012-03-15","2012-02-24","2012-02-20",35,{"copyrightStatement":815,"copyrightYear":816,"copyrightHolder":19,"licenseUrl":817,"licenseText":818},"Copyright © 2012 Cingolani, Patel, Coon, Nguyen, Land, Ruden and Lu.",2012,"http://www.frontiersin.org/licenseagreement",[819,820,832],{"#text":782},{"p":821},[822,824,830],{"#text":823},"This is an open-access article distributed under the terms of the ",{"uri":825,":@":828},[826],{"#text":827},"Creative Commons Attribution Non Commercial License",{"@_xlink:href":829},"http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/",{"#text":831},", which permits non-commercial use, distribution, and reproduction in other forums, provided the original authors and source are credited.",{"#text":788},[834,837,843,846],[835],{"#text":836},"personal genomes",[838,839,842],{"#text":782},{"italic":840},[841],{"#text":737},{"#text":788},[844],{"#text":845},"whole-genome SNP analysis",[847],{"#text":848},"next-generation DNA sequencing",[],[851,852,883],{"#text":788},{"p":853},[854,856,859,861,865,867,871,873,876,878,881],{"#text":855},"This paper describes a new program SnpSift for filtering differential DNA sequence variants between two or more experimental genomes after genotoxic chemical exposure. Here, we illustrate how SnpSift can be used to identify candidate phenotype-relevant variants including single nucleotide polymorphisms, multiple nucleotide polymorphisms, insertions, and deletions (InDels) in mutant strains isolated from genome-wide chemical mutagenesis of ",{"italic":857},[858],{"#text":737},{"#text":860},". First, the genomes of two independently isolated mutant fly strains that are allelic for a novel recessive male-sterile locus generated by genotoxic chemical exposure were sequenced using the Illumina next-generation DNA sequencer to obtain 20- to 29-fold coverage of the euchromatic sequences. The sequencing reads were processed and variants were called using standard bioinformatic tools. Next, SnpEff was used to annotate all sequence variants and their potential mutational effects on associated genes. Then, SnpSift was used to filter and select differential variants that potentially disrupt a common gene in the two allelic mutant strains. The potential causative DNA lesions were partially validated by capillary sequencing of polymerase chain reaction-amplified DNA in the genetic interval as defined by meiotic mapping and deletions that remove defined regions of the chromosome. Of the five candidate genes located in the genetic interval, the ",{"italic":862},[863],{"#text":864},"Pka-like",{"#text":866}," gene ",{"italic":868},[869],{"#text":870},"CG12069",{"#text":872}," was found to carry a separate pre-mature stop codon mutation in each of the two allelic mutants whereas the other four candidate genes within the interval have wild-type sequences. The ",{"italic":874},[875],{"#text":864},{"#text":877}," gene is therefore a strong candidate gene for the male-sterile locus. These results demonstrate that combining SnpEff and SnpSift can expedite the identification of candidate phenotype-causative mutations in chemically mutagenized ",{"italic":879},[880],{"#text":481},{"#text":882}," strains. This technique can also be used to characterize the variety of mutations generated by genotoxic chemicals.",{"#text":884},"\n      ",[886,888,1032,1033,2091,2092,2273,2274,2526,2527,2541,2542,2556],{"#text":887},"\n    ",{"sec":889,":@":1029,"anchorId":1031},[890,891,895,896,909,910,943,944,948,949,967,968,1028],{"#text":884},{"title":892},[893],{"#text":894},"Introduction",{"#text":884},{"p":897},[898,900,907],{"#text":899},"There are two types of chemicals that cause developmental abnormities in organisms – genotoxic chemicals and non-genotoxic chemicals. Genotoxic chemicals directly alkylate or oxidize the DNA and cause inappropriate base pairing. This causes permanent genetic mutations after exposing germline cells to genotoxic chemicals. Non-genotoxic chemicals are thought to cause epigenetic changes in the DNA that cause developmental abnormalities. Most non-genotoxic chemicals only affect development or the health of the organism exposed, but some non-genotoxic chemicals such as the estrogenic chemical diethylstilbestrol (DES) can cause developmental abnormalities and increased susceptibility to cancer for several generations (reviewed in Ruden et al., ",{"xref":901,":@":904},[902],{"#text":903},"2005",{"@_ref-type":905,"@_rid":906},"bibr","B17",{"#text":908},").",{"#text":884},{"p":911},[912,914,917,919,925,927,933,935,941],{"#text":913},"Random mutagenesis such as chemical mutagenesis with the genotoxic chemical ethyl methane sulfonate (EMS) is an incredibly powerful tool for generating mutant strains of cells or organisms for purposes of studying all types of biological processes. In mutant bacteria or yeast, identification of the mutated genes is often done by transforming wild-type DNA into the cells and screening for rescue of the mutant phenotype. One could then sequence the DNA that rescues the phenotype to find the gene mutated. In ",{"italic":915},[916],{"#text":737},{"#text":918},", a causative DNA lesion for an observable phenotype is traditionally done by meiotic mapping of the mutant locus using a series of visible genetic markers that span the chromosome (Anderson, ",{"xref":920,":@":923},[921],{"#text":922},"1992",{"@_ref-type":905,"@_rid":924},"B1",{"#text":926},"). Deficiencies that delete defined regions of the chromosome, typically tens to hundreds of kilobases long, can then be used to further refine the boundaries of the mutated gene locus (Parks et al., ",{"xref":928,":@":931},[929],{"#text":930},"2004",{"@_ref-type":905,"@_rid":932},"B12",{"#text":934},"; Ryder et al., ",{"xref":936,":@":939},[937],{"#text":938},"2007",{"@_ref-type":905,"@_rid":940},"B18",{"#text":942},"). However, these positional cloning techniques are not only labor-intensive and time consuming, but also without a guarantee of success. This frequently leads to inevitable delays in molecular and functional characterization of the gene involved, even in the post genomic era.",{"#text":884},{"p":945},[946],{"#text":947},"With the development of next-generation DNA sequencing instruments, whole-genome sequencing is becoming feasible to replace labor-intensive positional cloning methods. However, we are limited by the capacity of the current bioinformatic programs to rapidly and reliably process sequence variants including single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs), multiple nucleotide polymorphisms (MNPs), insertions, and deletions (InDels) between the wild-type control and the mutant genomes. This is especially the case in dealing with mutant strains isolated from random chemical mutagenesis that typically introduces quite large numbers of background sequence variants and SNPs into the mutant genome, only one of which is likely responsible for the mutant phenotype.",{"#text":884},{"p":950},[951,953,957,959,965],{"#text":952},"Furthermore, all current next-generation sequencers produce frequent errors, especially when approaching the 3′-ends of each short read. Using current technologies, a short read is typically 70–150 bp long. As the euchromatic genome of ",{"italic":954},[955],{"#text":956},"D. melanogaster",{"#text":958}," is 117 million base pairs (Mbp), machine-generated errors by themselves are sufficient to produce thousands of false SNPs in whole-genome sequencing data. To expedite the analyses of whole-genome sequencing data and to reduce number of false positives, we have developed the programs SnpEff (Pablo Cingolani and Douglas M. Ruden; Fly, in press; Platts et al., ",{"xref":960,":@":963},[961],{"#text":962},"2009",{"@_ref-type":905,"@_rid":964},"B14",{"#text":966},") and SnpSift. These programs can categorize and filter thousands of variants per second, based on their locations in the transcriptional unit and potential mutational effects on transcription or translation. By comparing several sequencing experiments, the number of false positives can be reduced.",{"#text":884},{"p":969},[970,972,975,977,983,985,991,993,1002,1004,1012,1014,1017,1019,1026],{"#text":971},"Whole-genome sequencing to identify a causative SNP has not been established for ",{"italic":973},[974],{"#text":956},{"#text":976}," mutants (Hillier et al., ",{"xref":978,":@":981},[979],{"#text":980},"2008",{"@_ref-type":905,"@_rid":982},"B5",{"#text":984},"; Wang et al., ",{"xref":986,":@":989},[987],{"#text":988},"2010",{"@_ref-type":905,"@_rid":990},"B19",{"#text":992},"). Here, we describe how SnpEff",{"xref":994,":@":999},[995],{"sup":996},[997],{"#text":998},"1",{"@_ref-type":1000,"@_rid":1001},"fn","fn1",{"#text":1003}," and SnpSift",{"xref":1005,":@":1010},[1006],{"sup":1007},[1008],{"#text":1009},"2",{"@_ref-type":1000,"@_rid":1011},"fn2",{"#text":1013}," can be used together to identify causative gene candidate using just two alleles of a male-sterile ",{"italic":1015},[1016],{"#text":481},{"#text":1018}," locus. Both programs have web based interfaces available via the Galaxy project",{"xref":1020,":@":1024},[1021],{"sup":1022},[1023],{"#text":638},{"@_ref-type":1000,"@_rid":1025},"fn3",{"#text":1027},".",{"#text":887},{"@_sec-type":1030},"introduction","h2",{"#text":887},{"sec":1034,"anchorId":2090},[1035,1036,1040,1041,1377,1378,1938,1939,2089],{"#text":884},{"title":1037},[1038],{"#text":1039},"Results",{"#text":884},{"sec":1042},[1043,1044,1048,1049,1181,1182,1247,1248,1280,1281,1309,1310,1376],{"#text":788},{"title":1045},[1046],{"#text":1047},"Whole-genome sequencing of male-sterile mutants X1 and X2",{"#text":788},{"p":1050},[1051,1053,1056,1058,1064,1066,1072,1074,1082,1084,1088,1090,1093,1095,1101,1102,1105,1107,1113,1115,1118,1120,1123,1125,1131,1132,1135,1137,1140,1142,1145,1147,1150,1152,1163,1165,1169,1171,1174,1176,1179],{"#text":1052},"Two allelic male-sterile mutations, X1 and X2, were identified in a F",{"sub":1054},[1055],{"#text":638},{"#text":1057}," genetic screen (Yang et al., ",{"xref":1059,":@":1062},[1060],{"#text":1061},"2011",{"@_ref-type":905,"@_rid":1063},"B20",{"#text":1065},"). Briefly, males isogenic for the third chromosome were fed the chemical mutagen ethyl methane sulfonate (EMS) for 12 h (10 mM in 1% sucrose solution; Ruden et al., ",{"xref":1067,":@":1070},[1068],{"#text":1069},"1997",{"@_ref-type":905,"@_rid":1071},"B15",{"#text":1073},") and then mated with virgin females of the genotype ",{"italic":1075},[1076,1078],{"#text":1077},"w",{"sup":1079},[1080],{"#text":1081},"1118",{"#text":1083},"; ",{"italic":1085},[1086],{"#text":1087},"TM2/TM6,Sb",{"#text":1089},". Approximately 10,000 of the F",{"sub":1091},[1092],{"#text":998},{"#text":1094}," males (",{"italic":1096},[1097,1098],{"#text":1077},{"sup":1099},[1100],{"#text":1081},{"#text":1083},{"sup":1103},[1104],{"#text":758},{"#text":1106},"/TM2 or ",{"italic":1108},[1109,1110],{"#text":1077},{"sup":1111},[1112],{"#text":1081},{"#text":1114},": ",{"sup":1116},[1117],{"#text":758},{"#text":1119},"/TM6, Sb; ",{"sup":1121},[1122],{"#text":758},{"#text":1124}," represents the mutagenized third chromosome) were then mated individually to ",{"italic":1126},[1127,1128],{"#text":1077},{"sup":1129},[1130],{"#text":1081},{"#text":1083},{"italic":1133},[1134],{"#text":1087},{"#text":1136}," virgin females to generate ~6,000 lines, each carrying a mutagenized third chromosome. From the F",{"sub":1138},[1139],{"#text":638},{"#text":1141}," flies, males homozygous for the mutagenized chromosome (",{"sup":1143},[1144],{"#text":758},{"#text":1146},"/",{"sup":1148},[1149],{"#text":758},{"#text":1151},") were tested for low fertility by crossing to virgin females from a wild-type stock (",{"italic":1153},[1154,1156,1159,1160],{"#text":1155},"y",{"sup":1157},[1158],{"#text":998},{"#text":1077},{"sup":1161},[1162],{"#text":998},{"#text":1164},"). From this genetic screen, approximately 50 lines were saved that have low male fertility. They were placed into complementation groups by crossing to each other in ~1,275 crosses (i.e., 1,275 = ",{"italic":1166},[1167],{"#text":1168},"N",{"#text":1170},"(",{"italic":1172},[1173],{"#text":1168},{"#text":1175}," + 1)/2, where ",{"italic":1177},[1178],{"#text":1168},{"#text":1180}," = 50). The characterization of two alleles of the same complementation group that we call X1 and X2 are presented. Details of the other male-sterile mutations isolated in the screen and phenotypic analyses of X1 and X2 will be presented elsewhere.",{"#text":788},{"p":1183},[1184,1186,1193,1195,1201,1203,1206,1209,1210,1214,1217,1221,1224,1228,1231,1233,1238,1240,1245],{"#text":1185},"Males homozygous for X1 and X2 were sequenced (see ",{"xref":1187,":@":1190},[1188],{"#text":1189},"Materials and Methods",{"@_ref-type":1191,"@_rid":1192},"sec","s1",{"#text":1194},"), producing over 90 million combined sequencing reads (~76 bp per read), ~10% of which are of insufficient quality and discarded. The remaining sequence reads represent approximately 20- to 29-fold coverage of the euchromatic DNA (Figure ",{"xref":1196,":@":1198},[1197],{"#text":998},{"@_ref-type":1199,"@_rid":1200},"fig","F1",{"#text":1202},"). These unique sequence reads were aligned to the reference genome (",{"italic":1204},[1205],{"#text":1155},{"sup":1207},[1208],{"#text":998},{"#text":1083},{"italic":1211},[1212],{"#text":1213},"cn",{"sup":1215},[1216],{"#text":998},{"italic":1218},[1219],{"#text":1220},"bw",{"sup":1222},[1223],{"#text":998},{"italic":1225},[1226],{"#text":1227},"sp",{"sup":1229},[1230],{"#text":998},{"#text":1232}," strain, dm5.30), variant calls were performed, and 204,250 homozygous SNPs were found with a quality score greater than 70 (Figure ",{"xref":1234,":@":1236},[1235],{"#text":1009},{"@_ref-type":1199,"@_rid":1237},"F2",{"#text":1239},"). There were also 97,574 heterozygous SNPs, but they were not analyzed further because the sequenced genomic DNA samples were purified from the X1/X1 and X2/X2 homozygous flies. We found that greater than 99.99% of the homozygous SNPs were identical for X1 and X2 and these have to be common background variants because X1 and X2 were derived from the same parental strain. The remaining SNPs differ between X1 and X2 and they are associated with 141 genes, which were examined further (Figure ",{"xref":1241,":@":1243},[1242],{"#text":638},{"@_ref-type":1199,"@_rid":1244},"F3",{"#text":1246},", see below).",{"#text":788},{"fig":1249,":@":1278},[1250,1251,1255,1256,1271,1272,1277],{"#text":782},{"label":1252},[1253],{"#text":1254},"Figure 1",{"#text":782},{"caption":1257},[1258,1260,1270],{"#text":1259},"\n            ",{"p":1261},[1262,1264,1268],{"#text":1263},"\n              ",{"bold":1265},[1266],{"#text":1267},"Mapping X1 to the reference genome",{"#text":1269},". The reference genome used was the latest FlyBase version (dm5.30). The quality score was arbitrarily set at 70 and above for this table. The numbers indicate the numbers of reads mapped to the indicated genomic region. U, unmapped regions. Het, heterochromatic regions.",{"#text":782},{"#text":782},{"graphic":1273,":@":1274},[],{"@_xmlns:xlink":1275,"@_xlink:href":1276},"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink","fgene-03-00035-g001.tif",{"#text":788},{"@_id":1200,"@_position":1279},"float",{"#text":788},{"fig":1282,":@":1308},[1283,1284,1288,1289,1302,1303,1307],{"#text":782},{"label":1285},[1286],{"#text":1287},"Figure 2",{"#text":782},{"caption":1290},[1291,1292,1301],{"#text":1259},{"p":1293},[1294,1295,1299],{"#text":1263},{"bold":1296},[1297],{"#text":1298},"Single nucleotide polymorphism calling for X1 SNPs with a quality score greater than or equal to 70",{"#text":1300},". We performed SNP calling using Samtools, which produced 1,943,047 SNPs with a quality score > 1. Out of these, 1,036,435 are homozygous SNPs. The low quality SNPs were filtered out using an arbitrary threshold of 70 (the peak of the distribution) leaving 204,205 homozygous SNPs. In order to identify the phenotype-causative candidate SNPs, we first assumed that they change an amino acid, splice site, reading frame, start or stop codon since these types of SNPs potentially alter the activity of the protein produced (we call these class 1 SNPs). Other types of SNPs such as intronic, intergenic, 5′UTR, 3′UTR, upstream, and downstream are less likely to affect gene function and they are considered secondarily only if no candidate genes could be identified from the first category of SNPs (we call these class 2 SNPs). Second, we considered the differential SNPs that are unique to either X1 or X2, but not common for X1 and X2 (Figure ",{"xref":1397,":@":1399},[1398],{"#text":638},{"@_ref-type":1199,"@_rid":1244},{"#text":1401},"A). The way that the male-sterile screen was conducted ensured that X1 and X2 carried independently mutagenized chromosomes, so it is very unlikely that they have identical phenotype-causative SNPs (see ",{"xref":1403,":@":1405},[1404],{"#text":1189},{"@_ref-type":1191,"@_rid":1192},{"#text":1407},"). For this analysis, thresholds above a certain level, such as 70, were not used because we did not want to eliminate a candidate SNP because it fell below an arbitrary threshold. For Figure ",{"xref":1415,":@":1417},[1416],{"#text":1009},{"@_ref-type":1199,"@_rid":1237},{"#text":1419},", for illustrative purposes, we used a threshold score of 70, based on the quality score distribution for this sequencing run (McCarthy, ",{"xref":1421,":@":1423},[1422],{"#text":988},{"@_ref-type":905,"@_rid":1424},"B11",{"#text":1426},"). Quality score, is defined by SAMtools as the probability of error in decibels, that is ",{"italic":1428},[1429],{"#text":1430},"q",{"#text":1432}," = −10 log(",{"italic":1434},[1435],{"#text":1436},"p",{"#text":1438},"), where ",{"italic":1440},[1441],{"#text":1436},{"#text":1443}," is the error probability and the logarithm is in base 10. Typically range for quality scores is from 1 to 100 with the higher score having a greater probability of being a real SNP and, therefore, not a sequencing artifact (McCarthy, ",{"xref":1445,":@":1447},[1446],{"#text":988},{"@_ref-type":905,"@_rid":1424},{"#text":908},{"#text":788},{"p":1451},[1452,1454,1458,1460,1464,1466,1470,1471,1474,1475,1479,1480,1484,1485,1489,1490,1494,1496,1500,1502,1506,1508,1512,1514,1517,1519,1523,1525,1529,1531,1534,1536,1540,1542,1548],{"#text":1453},"Next, we analyzed only the class 1 SNPs on the chromosome 3 since the X1 and X2 mutant strains were generated by using the third chromosome balancer (Figure ",{"xref":1455,":@":1457},[1456],{"#text":638},{"@_ref-type":1199,"@_rid":1244},{"#text":1459},"B). As a general exercise, we did not begin our analysis by focusing on the third chromosome alone because this may not be applicable to other experimental settings. X1 contains a TGG/TGA SNP that converts the tryptophan (W) residue 308 to a stop codon whereas X2 contains a CAG/TAG SNP that converts the glutamine (Q) residue 9 to a stop codon (Figure ",{"xref":1960,":@":1962},[1961],{"#text":1545},{"@_ref-type":1199,"@_rid":1547},{"#text":1964},"B). However, there is a poor match to the Kozak consensus sequence, 5′-ACC-ATG-G-3′, flanking the downstream ATG site, 5′-CAG-ATG-C-3′. Since a good match to the Kozak sequence is generally required for efficient translation, (Kozak, ",{"xref":1978,":@":1981},[1979],{"#text":1980},"1987",{"@_ref-type":905,"@_rid":1982},"B6",{"#text":1984},") it is possible that the downstream ATG is not used for translation. No SNPs were found in the remaining four candidate genes expressed in the testes (",{"italic":2043},[2044],{"#text":2045},"CG12066",{"#text":1465},{"italic":2048},[2049],{"#text":2050},"CG31010",{"#text":1465},{"italic":2053},[2054],{"#text":2055},"CG1340",{"#text":1465},{"italic":2058},[2059],{"#text":2060},"CG15543",{"#text":2062},"), suggesting that ",{"italic":2064},[2065],{"#text":870},{"#text":2067}," is a strong candidate gene for the sperm storage defects of X1 and X2.",{"#text":788},{"p":2070},[2071,2073,2076,2078,2081,2083,2086],{"#text":2072},"To further confirm the SNPs identified by SnpEff and SnpSift, genomic DNA samples were isolated from X1 and X2 homozygous mutant males and regions containing exons were amplified by polymerase chain reaction (PCR), cloned into pGEMT (Promega), and sequenced by capillary DNA sequencing (Applied Biosystems, Inc.). Sequencing confirmed the presence of stop codon SNPs in ",{"italic":2074},[2075],{"#text":870},{"#text":2077}," in both X1 and X2 at the expected locations. Thus, we conclude that the male-sterile alleles of X1 and X2 probably contain mutations in the ",{"italic":2079},[2080],{"#text":870},{"#text":2082}," gene. Complete validation will require a ",{"italic":2084},[2085],{"#text":870},{"#text":2087}," rescue transgene that is expressed in the male testes. We performed whole-genome shotgun sequencing of the two non-complementing alleles, X1 and X2, and showed that only a single gene, ",{"italic":2105},[2106],{"#text":1903},{"#text":2108},", was affected by SNPs at two different places, generating two different truncated proteins. From these, we conclude that a single lane of next-generation sequencing on the GAIIx instrument is probably sufficient for identifying homozygous causative SNP candidates in ",{"italic":2110},[2111],{"#text":481},{"#text":2113},". This tool set works almost exclusively on variant call format (VCF) files according to the specification for versions 4 or 4.1 (Danecek et al., ",{"xref":2423,":@":2425},[2424],{"#text":1061},{"@_ref-type":905,"@_rid":2426},"B4",{"#text":2428},"). The two main components used in this work were “SnpSift caseControl” and “SnpSift filter.” Frequently asked questions (FAQs) are addressed on our web site.",{"#text":788},{"#text":788},{"sec":2432},[2433,2434,2438,2439,2448],{"#text":782},{"title":2435},[2436],{"#text":2437},"SnpSift caseControl",{"#text":782},{"p":2440},[2441,2443,2446],{"#text":2442},"This tool counts the number of genotypes present in two user-defined groups (“case” and “control”), and then it calculates a ",{"italic":2444},[2445],{"#text":1436},{"#text":2447},"-value based on Fisher exact test. For each group, either homozygous, heterozygous, or both kinds of variants can be used.",{"#text":788},{"#text":788},{"sec":2451},[2452,2453,2457,2458,2480,2481,2485,2486,2506,2507,2521],{"#text":782},{"title":2454},[2455],{"#text":2456},"SnpSift filter",{"#text":782},{"p":2459},[2460,2462,2465,2467,2472,2474,2478],{"#text":2461},"This module performs filtering based on arbitrary expressions. In order to be able to parse arbitrary expressions, we created a top-down recursive grammar [also known as LL(",{"sup":2463},[2464],{"#text":758},{"#text":2466},") grammar] using ANTLR (Parr, ",{"xref":2468,":@":2470},[2469],{"#text":938},{"@_ref-type":905,"@_rid":2471},"B13",{"#text":2473},"). Using the lexer and parser created by ANTLR we are able to parse expressions by creating an abstract syntax tree (AST) for the expression. An AST is a well-known structure, very common in compiler design, that is used to represent the arbitrary input expressions from the user. The AST tree is converted into an ",{"italic":2475},[2476],{"#text":2477},"interpreter syntax tree (IST)",{"#text":2479},", which is a tree composed of objects capable of interpreting conditions, expressions, and functions. This means that the IST is like AST, but it is also capable of performing expression evaluation. The result of the filter expression is the value of the root node in the IST.",{"#text":782},{"p":2482},[2483],{"#text":2484},"There are well-known variables pre-defined according to the VCF format specification. Other additional variables and their respective data types are parsed from VCF meta-information in the file header. As specified in the norm, INFO meta-information lines define the type and the number of values (e.g., an array) in each INFO sub-field. Automatic variable conversion is implemented (e.g., INT is automatically converted to FLOAT whenever required). Genotype fields are similarly parsed by using FORMAT meta-information header lines.",{"#text":782},{"p":2487},[2488,2490,2496,2498,2504],{"#text":2489},"Each VCF entry (i.e., each non-header line in a VCF file) is converted into a set of “variable = value” tuples, which are feed into the interpreter tree. The IST, created using the user expression, interprets the user-defined expression from top to bottom trying to assign a Boolean value to the root node. If the result from evaluating the IST is “true” then the VCF line is either printed to standard output or marked as PASS in the FILTER field; likewise, if it is “false,” the line is filtered out (i.e., not printed) or marked as failed in the FILTER field.
